<p><b>Strepsirrhini</b> or <b>Strepsirhini</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/En-us-Strepsirrhini.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" data-file-height="11" data-file-width="11" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Strepsirrhini.ogg" title="File:En-us-Strepsirrhini.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/aɪ/ long 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">STREP</span></span></b>-sə-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">RY</span></span></b>-nee</i></a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">suborder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> that includes the <a href="/wiki/Lemuriformes" title="Lemuriformes">lemuriform</a> primates, which consist of the <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemurs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Madagascar" title="Fauna of Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galagos</a> ("bushbabies") and <a href="/wiki/Potto" title="Potto">pottos</a> from <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Africa" title="Fauna of Africa">Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Loris" title="Loris">lorises</a> from <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_India" title="Fauna of India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">southeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemuriformes_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lemuriformes-4">[a]</a></sup> Collectively they are referred to as strepsirrhines. Also belonging to the suborder are the extinct <a href="/wiki/Adapiformes" title="Adapiformes">adapiform</a> primates, a diverse and widespread group that thrived during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> (56 to 34 million years ago [<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>]) in Europe, North America, and Asia, but disappeared from most of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> as the climate cooled; the last of the adapiforms died out at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> (~7 mya). Adapiforms are sometimes referred to as being "lemur-like", although the diversity of both lemurs and adapiforms does not support this comparison. The two leading <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> classifications for the suborder divide living strepsirrhine primates into either two <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">superfamilies</a> (Lemuroidea and Lorisoidea) within the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">infraorder</a> Lemuriformes or two infraorders, Lemuriformes and Lorisiformes. The suborder represents a related group, and replaced the widely used and now obsolete suborder Prosimii ("<a href="/wiki/Prosimian" title="Prosimian">prosimians</a>"), which included strepsirrhines and <a href="/wiki/Tarsier" title="Tarsier">tarsiers</a>, a grouping based primarily on shared <a href="/wiki/Primate#Anatomy.2C_physiology.2C_and_morphology" title="Primate">anatomical</a> traits. Today, Strepsirrhini excludes the tarsiers, which are now grouped in the other major primate suborder, <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">Haplorhini</a>, along with the monkeys and apes (<a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">simians</a> or anthropoids). Strepsirrhines are often inappropriately referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Living_fossil" title="Living fossil">living fossils</a>". Instead, they have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> for millions of years under <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, and have diversified to fill many <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a>. Some of their traits may be derived from <a href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">ancestral</a> primates, while others are unique to strepsirrhines.</p>
<p>Strepsirrhines are defined by their wet nose or <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">rhinarium</a>. They also have a smaller brain than comparably sized simians, large <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory lobes</a> for smell, a <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a> to detect <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Bicornuate_uterus" title="Bicornuate uterus">bicornuate uterus</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Placentation" title="Placentation">epitheliochorial placenta</a>. Their eyes contain a <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">reflective layer</a> to improve their <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy)" title="Orbit (anatomy)">eye sockets</a> include a <a href="/wiki/Postorbital_bar" title="Postorbital bar">ring of bone</a> around the eye, but they lack a wall of thin bone behind it. Strepsirrhine primates produce their own <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, whereas haplorhine primates must obtain it from their diets. Lemuriform primates are characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Toothcomb" title="Toothcomb">toothcomb</a>, a specialized set of teeth in the front, lower part of the mouth mostly used for combing fur during <a href="/wiki/Personal_grooming" title="Personal grooming">grooming</a>. Often, the toothcomb is incorrectly used to characterize all strepsirrhines. Instead, it is unique to lemuriforms and is not seen among adapiforms. Lemuriforms groom orally, and also possess a <a href="/wiki/Grooming_claw" title="Grooming claw">grooming claw</a> on the second toe of each foot for scratching in areas that are inaccessible to the mouth and tongue. It is unclear whether adapiforms possessed grooming claws.</p>
<p>The taxonomy of strepsirrhines is controversial and has a complicated history. Confused taxonomic terminology and oversimplified anatomical comparisons have created misconceptions about primate and strepsirrhine <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a>, illustrated by the media attention surrounding the single <a href="/wiki/Darwinius" title="Darwinius">"Ida" fossil</a> in 2009. Strepsirrhines diverged from the haplorhine primates near the beginning of the primate <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">radiation</a> between 55 and 90 mya. Older divergence dates are based on <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">genetic analysis</a> estimates, while younger dates are based on the scarce <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil record</a>. Lemuriform primates may have evolved from either <a href="/wiki/Cercamoniinae" title="Cercamoniinae">cercamoniines</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sivaladapidae" title="Sivaladapidae">sivaladapids</a>, both of which were adapiforms that may have originated in Asia. They were once thought to have evolved from <a href="/wiki/Adapidae" title="Adapidae">adapids</a>, a more specialized and younger branch of adapiform primarily from Europe. Lemurs <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">rafted</a> from Africa to Madagascar between 47 and 54 mya, whereas the lorises split from the African galagos around 40 mya and later colonized Asia. Both living and extinct strepsirrhines are behaviorally diverse, although all are primarily <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreal</a> (tree-dwelling). Most living lemuriforms are <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a>, while most extinct adapiforms were <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a>. Both living and extinct groups primarily fed on <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">fruit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folivore" title="Folivore">leaves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insects</a>. Many of today's strepsirrhines are <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, hunting for <a href="/wiki/Bushmeat" title="Bushmeat">bushmeat</a>, and live capture for the <a href="/wiki/Exotic_pet" title="Exotic pet">exotic pet</a> trade.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> name Strepsirrhini derives from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> στρεψίς (<i>strepsis</i> or "a turning [inward])" and ῥινός (<i>rhinos</i> or "nose"), which refers to the appearance of the sinuous (comma-shaped) nostrils on the <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">rhinarium</a> or wet nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007394_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007394-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup> The name was first used by French <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">naturalist</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Geoffroy_Saint-Hilaire" title="Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire">Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire</a> in 1812 as a <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">subordinal</a> rank comparable to Platyrrhini (<a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">New World monkeys</a>) and Catarrhini (<a href="/wiki/Old_World_monkey" title="Old World monkey">Old World monkeys</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195339_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195339-7">[6]</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">description</a>, he mentioned "<i>Les narines terminales et sinueuses</i>" ("Nostrils terminal and winding").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812156_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812156-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>When British <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoologist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reginald_Innes_Pocock" title="Reginald Innes Pocock">Reginald Innes Pocock</a> revived Strepsirrhini and defined <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">Haplorhini</a> in 1918, he omitted the second "r" from both ("Strepsi<u>r</u>hini" and "Haplo<u>r</u>hini" instead of "Strepsi<u>rr</u>hini" and "Haplo<u>rr</u>hini"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves2008166_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves2008166-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPocock191851_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPocock191851-10">[9]</a></sup> although he did not remove the second "r" from Platyrrhini or Catarrhini, both of which were also named by É. Geoffroy in 1812. Following Pocock, many researchers continued to spell Strepsirrhini with a single "r" until <a href="/wiki/Primatology" title="Primatology">primatologists</a> Paulina Jenkins and Prue Napier pointed out the error in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJenkinsNapier19871_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJenkinsNapier19871-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;">Strepsirrhines and haplorhines diverged shortly after the emergence of the first true primates (euprimates). The relationship between euprimates, <a href="/wiki/Treeshrew" title="Treeshrew">treeshrews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colugo" title="Colugo">colugos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plesiadapiformes" title="Plesiadapiformes">plesiadapiforms</a> is less certain. Sometimes plesiadapiforms are grouped with the euprimates under the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> Primates, colugos are grouped with primates under <a href="/wiki/Primatomorpha" title="Primatomorpha">Primatomorpha</a>, and all four are grouped under <a href="/wiki/Euarchonta" title="Euarchonta">Euarchonta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006157_.26_165.E2.80.93167_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006157_.26_165.E2.80.93167-12">[11]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Strepsirrhines include the extinct adapiforms and the <a href="/wiki/Lemuriformes" title="Lemuriformes">lemuriform</a> primates, which include lemurs and lorisoids (<a href="/wiki/Loris" title="Loris">lorises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potto" title="Potto">pottos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galagos</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166-13">[12]</a></sup> The lemuriforms, and particularly the lemurs of Madagascar, are often portrayed inappropriately as "<a href="/wiki/Living_fossil" title="Living fossil">living fossils</a>" or as examples of "<a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a>", or "inferior" primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009397_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009397-16">[15]</a></sup> These views have historically hindered the understanding of <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_mammals" title="Evolution of mammals">mammalian evolution</a> and the evolution of strepsirrhine traits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322-14">[13]</a></sup> such as their reliance on smell (<a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfaction</a>), characteristics of their skeletal anatomy, and their brain size, which is relatively small. In the case of lemurs, <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> has driven this isolated population of primates to diversify significantly and fill a rich variety of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a>, despite their smaller and less complex brains compared to simians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009397_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009397-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The origin of the earliest primates, from which both the strepsirrhines and haplorhines (simians and tarsiers) evolved, is a mystery. Both their place of origin and the group from which they evolved are uncertain. Although the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil record</a> demonstrating their initial <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation" title="Evolutionary radiation">radiation</a> across the Northern Hemisphere is very detailed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006168.E2.80.93169_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006168.E2.80.93169-17">[16]</a></sup> the fossil record from the tropics—where primates most likely evolved—is very poor, particularly around the time that primates and other major <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a> (groups consisting of an ancestor and all its descendants) of <a href="/wiki/Eutheria" title="Eutheria">eutherian</a> mammals were first appearing. Consequently, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">geneticists</a> and primatologists have used genetic analyses to determine the relatedness between primate lineages and the amount of time since they <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">diverged</a>. Using this <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a>, divergence dates for the major primate lineages have suggested that primates evolved more than 80–90 mya, nearly 40 million years before the first primates appear in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1400" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg/220px-Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg/330px-Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg/440px-Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loris_lydekkerianus_nordicus_003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Early primates possessed adaptations for <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreal locomotion</a> that enabled maneuvering along fine branches, as seen in this <a href="/wiki/Slender_loris" title="Slender loris">slender loris</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The early primates include both <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> small-bodied species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201124_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201124-19">[18]</a></sup> and all were arboreal, with hands and feet specially adapted for maneuvering on small branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201184_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201184-20">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plesiadapiformes" title="Plesiadapiformes">Plesiadapiforms</a> from the early <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> are sometimes considered "archaic primates" because their teeth resembled those of early primates, and because they possessed arboreal adaptations, such as a divergent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hallux" title="Hallux">hallux</a> (big toe). Although plesiadapiforms were closely related to primates, they may represent a <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> group from which primates may or may not have directly evolved,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006169_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006169-21">[20]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> may have been more closely related to <a href="/wiki/Colugo" title="Colugo">colugos</a>, also known as dermopterans or "flying lemurs", which are thought to be closely related to primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006165_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006165-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first true primates (euprimates) do not appear in the fossil record until the early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> (~55 mya), at which point they radiated across the Northern Hemisphere during a brief period of rapid <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene%E2%80%93Eocene_Thermal_Maximum" title="Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum">Paleocene–Eocene Thermal Maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006343_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006343-23">[22]</a></sup> These first primates included <i><a href="/wiki/Cantius" title="Cantius">Cantius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Donrussellia" title="Donrussellia">Donrussellia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Altanius" title="Altanius">Altanius</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Teilhardina" title="Teilhardina">Teilhardina</a></i> on the northern continents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillerGunnellMartin200567_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillerGunnellMartin200567-24">[23]</a></sup> as well as the more questionable (and fragmentary) fossil <i><a href="/wiki/Altiatlasius" title="Altiatlasius">Altiatlasius</a></i> from Paleocene Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128.E2.80.9329_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128.E2.80.9329-25">[24]</a></sup> These earliest <a href="/wiki/List_of_fossil_primates" title="List of fossil primates">fossil primates</a> are often divided into two groups, adapiforms (sometimes called adapids, or adapoids) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omomyiformes" title="Omomyiformes">omomyiforms</a> (or omomyids—suspected relatives of tarsiers). Both appeared suddenly in the fossil record without <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">transitional forms</a> to indicate ancestry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006178.E2.80.93179_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006178.E2.80.93179-26">[25]</a></sup> and both groups were rich in diversity and were widespread throughout the Eocene. Although few fossils of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> primate groups—lemuriforms, tarsiers, or simians—are known from the Early to Middle Eocene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeard2002133_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeard2002133-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimons200315.E2.80.9317_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimons200315.E2.80.9317-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446-29">[28]</a></sup> evidence from genetics and newer fossil finds suggest they may have been present during this early <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006343_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006343-23">[22]</a></sup> The divergence between strepsirrhines, simians, and tarsiers likely followed almost immediately after primates first evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128.E2.80.9329_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128.E2.80.9329-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adapiform_evolution">Adapiform evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Adapiform evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Notharctus_osborni.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4381" data-file-width="2456" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Notharctus_osborni.jpg/170px-Notharctus_osborni.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Notharctus_osborni.jpg/255px-Notharctus_osborni.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Notharctus_osborni.jpg/340px-Notharctus_osborni.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Notharctus_osborni.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Notharctus" title="Notharctus">Notharctus</a></i>, a type of North American adapiform, resembled lemurs but did not give rise to them.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adapiforms are extinct strepsirrhines that shared many anatomical similarities with lemuriforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup> They are sometimes referred to as lemur-like primates, although the diversity of both lemurs and adapiforms do not support this analogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200215.E2.80.9316_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200215.E2.80.9316-31">[30]</a></sup> Like living strepsirrhines, adapiforms were extremely diverse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322-14">[13]</a></sup> with at least 30 genera and 80 species known from the fossil record as of the early 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200221_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200221-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200214_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200214-33">[32]</a></sup> They diversified across <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasia</a> during the Eocene,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup> some reaching North America via a <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200235_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200235-34">[33]</a></sup>They were among the most common mammals found in the fossil beds from that time. A few rare species have also been found in northern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup> The most basal of the adapiforms include the genera <i>Cantius</i> from North America and Europe and <i>Donrussellia</i> from Europe. The latter bears the most <a href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">ancestral traits</a>, so it is often considered a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_group" title="Stem group">stem group</a> of the other adapiforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adapiforms are often divided into three major groups:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adapidae" title="Adapidae">Adapids</a> were most commonly found in Europe, although the oldest specimens (<i><a href="/wiki/Adapoides" title="Adapoides">Adapoides</a></i> from middle Eocene China) indicate that they most likely evolved in Asia and immigrated. They died out in Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene%E2%80%93Oligocene_extinction_event" title="Eocene–Oligocene extinction event">Grande Coupure</a>, part of a significant <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a> at the end of the Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006185.E2.80.93186_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006185.E2.80.93186-36">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Notharctidae" title="Notharctidae">Notharctids</a>, which most closely resembled some of Madagascar's lemurs, come from Europe and North America. The European branch is often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Cercamoniinae" title="Cercamoniinae">cercamoniines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182.E2.80.93185_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182.E2.80.93185-37">[36]</a></sup> The North American branch thrived during the Eocene, but did not survive into the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodinot1998239_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodinot1998239-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGunnellRoseRasmussen2008257_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGunnellRoseRasmussen2008257-39">[38]</a></sup> Like the adapids, the European branch were also extinct by the end of the Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200235_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200235-34">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sivaladapidae" title="Sivaladapidae">Sivaladapids</a> of southern and eastern Asia are best known from the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, and the only adapiforms to survive past the Eocene/Oligocene boundary (~34 mya). Their relationship to the other adapiforms remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006186_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006186-40">[39]</a></sup> They had vanished before the end of the Miocene (~7 mya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The relationship between adapiform and lemuriform primates has not been clearly demonstrated, so the position of adapiforms as a paraphyletic stem group is questionable. Both molecular clock data and new fossil finds suggest that the lemuriform divergence from the other primates and the subsequent lemur-lorisoid split both predate the appearance of adapiforms in the early Eocene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006178.E2.80.93179_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006178.E2.80.93179-26">[25]</a></sup> New calibration methods may reconcile the discrepancies between the molecular clock and the fossil record, favoring more recent divergence dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteiperSeiffert20126006_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteiperSeiffert20126006-41">[40]</a></sup> The fossil record suggests that the strepsirrhine adapiforms and the haplorhine omomyiforms had been evolving independently before the early Eocene, although their most basal members share enough dental similarities to suggest that they diverged during the Paleocene (66–55 mya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lemuriform_evolution">Lemuriform evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lemuriform evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_lemurs" title="Evolution of lemurs">Evolution of lemurs</a></div>
<p>Lemuriform origins are unclear and debated. American <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Philip_D._Gingerich" title="Philip D. Gingerich">Philip Gingerich</a> proposed that lemuriform primates evolved from one of several genera of European adapids based on similarities between the front lower teeth of adapids and the <a href="/wiki/Toothcomb" title="Toothcomb">toothcomb</a> of extant lemuriforms; however, this view is not strongly supported due to a lack of clear transitional fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_.26_186_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182_.26_186-42">[41]</a></sup> Instead, lemuriforms may be descended from a very early branch of Asian cercamoniines or sivaladapids that migrated to northern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETabuceMarivauxLebrunAdaci20094091.E2.80.934092_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETabuceMarivauxLebrunAdaci20094091.E2.80.934092-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until discoveries of three 40-million-year-old fossil lorisoids (<i><a href="/wiki/Karanisia" title="Karanisia">Karanisia</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saharagalago&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saharagalago (page does not exist)">Saharagalago</a></i>, and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wadilemur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wadilemur (page does not exist)">Wadilemur</a></i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Faiyum_Oasis" title="Faiyum Oasis">El Fayum deposits</a> of Egypt between 1997 and 2005, the oldest known lemuriforms had come from the early Miocene (~20 mya) of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>. These newer finds demonstrate that lemuriform primates were present during the middle Eocene in Afro-Arabia and that the lemuriform lineage and all other strepsirrhine taxa had diverged before then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201124.E2.80.9325_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201124.E2.80.9325-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006187_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006187-46">[45]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Djebelemur" title="Djebelemur">Djebelemur</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> dates to the late early or early middle Eocene (52 to 46 mya) and has been considered a cercamoniine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006185_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006185-47">[46]</a></sup> but also may have been a stem lemuriform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodinot2006446-29">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Azibiidae" title="Azibiidae">Azibiids</a> from <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> date to roughly the same time and may be a sister group of the <a href="/wiki/Djebelemuridae" title="Djebelemuridae">djebelemurids</a>. Together with <i><a href="/wiki/Plesiopithecus" title="Plesiopithecus">Plesiopithecus</a></i> from the late Eocene Egypt, the three may qualify as the stem lemuriforms from Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETabuceMarivauxLebrunAdaci20094091.E2.80.934092_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETabuceMarivauxLebrunAdaci20094091.E2.80.934092-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Molecular clock estimates indicate that lemurs and the lorisoids diverged in Africa during the Paleocene, approximately 62 mya. Between 47 and 54 mya, lemurs dispersed to Madagascar by <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_dispersal" title="Oceanic dispersal">rafting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171-45">[44]</a></sup> In isolation, the lemurs diversified and filled the niches often filled by monkeys and apes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> In Africa, the lorises and galagos diverged during the Eocene, approximately 40 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011170.E2.80.93171-45">[44]</a></sup> Unlike the lemurs in Madagascar, they have had to compete with monkeys and apes, as well as other mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomic_classification">Taxonomic classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Taxonomic classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geoffroy72.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1122" data-file-width="834" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Geoffroy72.jpg/170px-Geoffroy72.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Geoffroy72.jpg/255px-Geoffroy72.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Geoffroy72.jpg/340px-Geoffroy72.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geoffroy72.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The suborder Strepsirrhini was proposed by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Geoffroy_Saint-Hilaire" title="Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire">É. Geoffroy</a> in 1812.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Strepsirrhine primates were first grouped under the genus <i>Lemur</i> by Swedish taxonomist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a> published in 1758. At the time, only three species were recognized, one of which (the colugo) is no longer recognized as a primate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves200889.E2.80.9390_.26_96_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves200889.E2.80.9390_.26_96-49">[48]</a></sup> In 1785, Dutch naturalist <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Boddaert" title="Pieter Boddaert">Pieter Boddaert</a> divided the genus <i>Lemur</i> into two genera: <i>Prosimia</i> for the lemurs, colugos, and tarsiers and <i>Tardigradus</i> for the lorises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves2008103_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves2008103-50">[49]</a></sup> Ten years later, É. Geoffroy and <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Georges Cuvier</a> grouped the tarsiers and galagos due to similarities in their hindlimb <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>, a view supported by German zoologist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Karl_Wilhelm_Illiger" title="Johann Karl Wilhelm Illiger">Johann Karl Wilhelm Illiger</a>, who placed them in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> Macrotarsi while placing the lemurs and tarsiers in the family Prosimia (Prosimii) in 1811. The use of the tarsier-galago classification continued for many years until 1898, when Dutch zoologist <a href="/wiki/Ambrosius_Hubrecht" title="Ambrosius Hubrecht">Ambrosius Hubrecht</a> demonstrated two different types of <a href="/wiki/Placentation" title="Placentation">placentation</a> (formation of a <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>) in the two groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>English <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomist</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Flower" title="William Henry Flower">William Henry Flower</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">suborder</a> Lemuroidea in 1883 to distinguish these primates from the simians, which were grouped under English <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biologist</a> <a href="/wiki/St._George_Jackson_Mivart" title="St. George Jackson Mivart">St. George Jackson Mivart</a>'s suborder Anthropoidea (=Simiiformes). According to Flower, the suborder Lemuroidea contained the families Lemuridae (lemurs, lorises, and galagos), Chiromyidae (<a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">aye-aye</a>), and Tarsiidae (tarsiers). Lemuroidea was later replaced by Illiger's suborder Prosimii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354-51">[50]</a></sup> Many years earlier, in 1812, É. Geoffroy first named the suborder Strepsirrhini, in which he included the tarsiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves2008113.E2.80.93114_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves2008113.E2.80.93114-52">[51]</a></sup> This taxonomy went unnoticed until 1918, when Pocock compared the structure of the nose and reinstated the use of the suborder Strepsirrhini, while also moving the tarsiers and the simians into a new suborder, Haplorhini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves2008166_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves2008166-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354-51">[50]</a></sup> It was not until 1953, when British anatomist <a href="/wiki/William_Charles_Osman_Hill" title="William Charles Osman Hill">William Charles Osman Hill</a> wrote an entire volume on strepsirrhine anatomy, that Pocock's taxonomic suggestion became noticed and more widely used. Since then, primate taxonomy has shifted between Strepsirrhini-Haplorhini and Prosimii-Anthropoidea multiple times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200353.E2.80.9354-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the academic literature provides a basic framework for primate taxonomy, usually including several potential taxonomic schemes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120_.26_22_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120_.26_22-53">[52]</a></sup> Although most experts agree upon <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman200345_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman200345-54">[53]</a></sup> many disagree about nearly every level of primate classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120_.26_22_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120_.26_22-53">[52]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subordinal_controversies">Subordinal controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subordinal controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most commonly recurring debate in primatology during the 1970s, 1980s, and early 2000s concerned the phylogenetic position of tarsiers compared to both simians and the other prosimians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200354_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200354-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200796_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200796-56">[55]</a></sup> Tarsiers are most often placed in either the suborder Haplorhini with the simians or in the suborder Prosimii with the strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> Prosimii is one of the two traditional primate suborders and is based on <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_grade" title="Evolutionary grade">evolutionary grades</a> (groups united by anatomical traits) rather than phylogenetic clades, while the Strepsirrhini-Haplorrhini taxonomy was based on evolutionary relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup> Yet both systems persist because the Prosimii-Anthropoidea taxonomy is familiar and frequently seen in the research literature and textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Strepsirrhines are traditionally characterized by several <a href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">symplesiomorphic</a> (ancestral) traits not shared with the simians, particularly the rhinarium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128-59">[57]</a></sup> Other symplesiomorphies include long <a href="/wiki/Snout" title="Snout">snouts</a>, convoluted <a href="/wiki/Nasal_concha" title="Nasal concha">maxilloturbinals</a>, relatively large <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulbs</a>, and smaller brains. The toothcomb is a <a href="/wiki/Synapomorphy" title="Synapomorphy">synapomorphy</a> (shared, derived trait) seen among lemuriforms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> although it is frequently and incorrectly used to define the strepsirrhine clade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[c]</a></sup> Strepsirrhine primates are also united in possessing an <a href="/wiki/Placentation#Placentation_in_mammals" title="Placentation">epitheliochorial placenta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> Unlike the tarsiers and simians, strepsirrhines are capable of <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">producing</a> their own <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> and do not need it supplied in their diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchwartz200371_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchwartz200371-62">[59]</a></sup> Further genetic evidence for the relationship between tarsiers and simians as a haplorhine clade is the shared possession of three <a href="/wiki/Retrotransposon#SINEs" title="Retrotransposon">SINE markers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200732_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200732-63">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of their historically mixed assemblages which included tarsiers and close relatives of primates, both Prosimii and Strepsirrhini have been considered <a href="/wiki/Wastebasket_taxon" title="Wastebasket taxon">wastebasket taxa</a> for "lower primates".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmill201016.E2.80.9317_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmill201016.E2.80.9317-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasmussenNekaris1998252_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenNekaris1998252-65">[62]</a></sup> Regardless, the strepsirrhine and haplorrhine clades are generally accepted and viewed as the preferred taxonomic division. Yet tarsiers still closely resemble both strepsirrhines and simians in different ways,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201122-18">[17]</a></sup> and since the early split between strepsirrhines, tarsiers and simians is ancient and hard to resolve,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200731.E2.80.9332_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200731.E2.80.9332-66">[63]</a></sup> a third taxonomic arrangement with three suborders is sometimes used: Prosimii, Tarsiiformes, and Anthropoidea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166-13">[12]</a></sup> More often, the term "prosimian" is no longer used in official taxonomy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves199813_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves199813-67">[64]</a></sup> but is still used to illustrate the behavioral ecology of tarsiers relative to the other primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201128-59">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the controversy over tarsiers, the debate over the origins of simians once called the strepsirrhine clade into question. Arguments for an evolutionary link between adapiforms and simians made by paleontologists Gingerich, <a href="/wiki/Elwyn_L._Simons" title="Elwyn L. Simons">Elwyn L. Simons</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Tab_Rasmussen" title="David Tab Rasmussen">Tab Rasmussen</a>, and others could have potentially excluded adapiforms from Strepsirrhini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006167_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006167-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeard2002146.E2.80.93147_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeard2002146.E2.80.93147-69">[66]</a></sup> In 1975, Gingerich proposed a new suborder, Simiolemuriformes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGingerich1975164_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGingerich1975164-70">[67]</a></sup> to suggest that strepsirrhines are more closely related to simians than tarsiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoder2003161.E2.80.93162_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoder2003161.E2.80.93162-71">[68]</a></sup> However, no clear relationship between the two had been demonstrated by the early 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeard2002146.E2.80.93147_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeard2002146.E2.80.93147-69">[66]</a></sup> The idea reemerged briefly in 2009 during the media attention surrounding <i><a href="/wiki/Darwinius" title="Darwinius">Darwinius masillae</a></i> (dubbed "Ida"), a cercamoniine from Germany that was touted as a "<a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">missing link</a> between humans and earlier primates" (simians and adapiforms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567-72">[69]</a></sup> However, the cladistic analysis was flawed and the phylogenetic inferences and terminology were vague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567-72">[69]</a></sup> Although the authors noted that <i>Darwinius</i> was not a "fossil lemur", they did emphasize the absence of a toothcomb,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranzenGingerichHabersetzerHurum2009e5723_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranzenGingerichHabersetzerHurum2009e5723-73">[70]</a></sup> which adapiforms did not possess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infraordinal_classification_and_clade_terminology">Infraordinal classification and clade terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Infraordinal classification and clade terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Within Strepsirrhini, two common classifications include either two infraorders (Adapiformes and Lemuriformes)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190-74">[71]</a></sup> or three infraorders (Adapiformes, Lemuriformes, Lorisiformes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120-75">[72]</a></sup> A less common taxonomy places the aye-aye (Daubentoniidae) in its own infraorder, Chiromyiformes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves2005121_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves2005121-76">[73]</a></sup> In some cases, plesiadapiforms are included within the order Primates, in which case Euprimates is sometimes treated as a suborder, with Strepsirrhini becoming an infraorder, and the Lemuriformes and others become parvorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166-13">[12]</a></sup> Regardless of the infraordinal taxonomy, Strepsirrhini is composed of three ranked superfamilies and 14 families, seven of which are extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190-74">[71]</a></sup> Three of these extinct families included the recently extinct <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">giant lemurs</a> of Madagascar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers2002106_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers2002106-77">[74]</a></sup> many of which died out within the last 1,000 years following human arrival on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers200297_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers200297-78">[75]</a></sup></p>
