</div>
</div>
<p>When Strepsirrhini is divided into two infraorders, the clade containing all toothcombed primates can be called "lemuriforms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201190-74">[71]</a></sup> When it is divided into three infraorders, the term "lemuriforms" refers only to Madagascar's lemurs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> and the toothcombed primates are referred to as either "crown strepsirrhines"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006186_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006186-40">[39]</a></sup> or "extant strepsirrhines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup> Confusion of this specific terminology with the general term "strepsirrhine", along with oversimplified anatomical comparisons and vague phylogenetic inferences, can lead to misconceptions about primate phylogeny and misunderstandings about primates from the Eocene, as seen with the media coverage of <i>Darwinius</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsKayChristopher_KirkRoss2010567-72">[69]</a></sup> Because the skeletons of adapiforms share strong similarities with those of lemurs and lorises, researchers have often referred to them as "primitive" strepsirrhines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006181_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006181-79">[76]</a></sup> lemur ancestors, or a sister group to the living strepsirrhines. They are included in Strepsirrhini,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup> and are considered basal members of the clade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200218_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200218-80">[77]</a></sup> Although their status as true primates is not questioned, the questionable relationship between adapiforms and other living and fossil primates leads to multiple classifications within Strepsirrhini. Often, adapiforms are placed in their own infraorder due to anatomical differences with lemuriforms and their unclear relationship. When shared traits with lemuriforms (which may or may not be synapomorphic) are emphasized, they are sometimes reduced to families within the infraorder Lemuriformes (or superfamily Lemuroidea).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006179_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006179-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first fossil primate described was the adapiform <i><a href="/wiki/Adapis" title="Adapis">Adapis parisiensis</a></i> by French naturalist <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Georges Cuvier</a> in 1821,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006185_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006185-47">[46]</a></sup> who compared it to a <a href="/wiki/Hyrax" title="Hyrax">hyrax</a> ("<i>le Daman</i>"), then considered a member of a now obsolete group called <a href="/wiki/Pachydermata" title="Pachydermata">pachyderms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunkelZijlstraGroves201268_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunkelZijlstraGroves201268-81">[78]</a></sup> It was not recognized as a primate until it was reevaluated in the early 1870s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200221_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200221-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006185_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006185-47">[46]</a></sup> Originally, adapiforms were all included under the family Adapidae, which was divided into two or three subfamilies: Adapinae, Notharctinae, and sometimes Sivaladapinae. All North American adapiforms were lumped under Notharctinae, while the Old World forms were usually assigned to Adapinae. Around the 1990s, two distinct groups of European "adapids" began to emerge, based on differences in the <a href="/wiki/Postcrania" title="Postcrania">postcranial skeleton</a> and the teeth. One of these two European forms was identified as cercamoniines, which were allied with the notharctids found mostly in North America, while the other group falls into the traditional adapid classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup> The three major adapiform divisions are now typically regarded as three families within Adapiformes (Notharctidae, Adapidae and Sivaladapidae), but other divisions ranging from one to five families are used as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200221_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200221-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1418" data-file-width="2100" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/220px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/330px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg/440px-GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GarnettsGalago_CincinnatiZoo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Strepsirrhines have a reflective layer in the eye, called a <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a> that helps them see better at night.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All lemuriforms possess a specialized dental structure called a toothcomb,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994-82">[79]</a></sup> with the exception of the aye-aye, in which the structure has been modified into two continually growing (hypselodont) <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine teeth</a>), similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>. The toothcomb consists of either two or four <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/procumbent" title="wiktionary:procumbent">procumbent</a> lower incisors and procumbent lower canine teeth followed by a canine-shaped <a href="/wiki/Premolar" title="Premolar">premolar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECuozzoYamashita200673_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECuozzoYamashita200673-83">[80]</a></sup> It is used to comb the fur during oral grooming. Shed hairs that accumulate between the teeth of the toothcomb are removed by the <a href="/wiki/Sublingua" title="Sublingua">sublingua</a> or "under-tongue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396-84">[81]</a></sup> Lemuriforms also possess a <a href="/wiki/Grooming_claw" title="Grooming claw">grooming claw</a> on the second digit of each foot for scratching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396-84">[81]</a></sup> Adapiforms did not possess a toothcomb. Instead, their lower incisors varied in orientation—from somewhat procumbent to somewhat vertical—and the lower canines were projected upwards and were often prominent. Adapiforms may have had a grooming claw,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup> but there is little evidence of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200239_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200239-85">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like all primates, strepsirrhine orbits (eye sockets) have a <a href="/wiki/Postorbital_bar" title="Postorbital bar">postorbital bar</a>, a protective ring of bone created by a connection between the <a href="/wiki/Frontal_bone" title="Frontal bone">frontal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zygomatic_bone" title="Zygomatic bone">zygomatic bones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167-86">[83]</a></sup> Both living and extinct strepsirrhines lack a thin wall of bone behind the eye, referred to as postorbital closure, which is only seen in haplorhine primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167-86">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETattersall20067_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETattersall20067-87">[84]</a></sup> Although the eyes of strepsirrhines point forward, giving <a href="/wiki/Stereopsis" title="Stereopsis">stereoscopic vision</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006166.E2.80.93167-86">[83]</a></sup> the orbits do not face fully forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETattersall20067_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETattersall20067-87">[84]</a></sup> Among living strepsirrhines, most or all species are thought to possess a reflective layer behind the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of the eye, called a <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a> (consisting of <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a> crystals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOllivierSamuelsonBrooksLewis2004_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOllivierSamuelsonBrooksLewis2004-88">[85]</a></sup>), which improves vision in low light,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007458_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007458-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994-82">[79]</a></sup> but they lack a <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">fovea</a>, which improves day vision. This differs from tarsiers, which lack a tapetum lucidum but possess a fovea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19996_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19996-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eulemur_mongoz_(male_-_face).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg/220px-Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg/330px-Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg/440px-Eulemur_mongoz_%28male_-_face%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eulemur_mongoz_(male_-_face).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Strepsirrhines are characterized by a typically longer snout and wet nose compared to <a href="/wiki/Haplorhini" title="Haplorhini">haplorhine</a> primates.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Strepsirrhine primates have a brain relatively comparable to or slightly larger in size than most mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> Compared to simians, however, they have a relatively small brain-to-body size ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETattersall20067_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETattersall20067-87">[84]</a></sup> Strepsirrhines are also traditionally noted for their unfused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphysis_menti" title="Symphysis menti">mandibular symphysis</a> (two halves of the lower jaw),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETattersall20067_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETattersall20067-87">[84]</a></sup> however, fusion of the mandibular symphysis was common in adapiforms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006180_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006180-91">[88]</a></sup> notably <i>Notharctus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006184_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006184-92">[89]</a></sup> Also, several extinct giant lemurs exhibited a fused mandibular symphysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers2002106_.26_112_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodfreyJungers2002106_.26_112-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many nocturnal species have large, independently movable ears,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007429_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007429-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.935_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.935-95">[92]</a></sup> although there are significant differences in sizes and shapes of the ear between species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007431_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007431-96">[93]</a></sup> The structure of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">inner ear</a> of strepsirrhines differs between the lemurs and lorisoids. In lemurs, the <a href="/wiki/Tympanic_cavity" title="Tympanic cavity">tympanic cavity</a>, which surrounds the middle ear, is expanded. This leaves the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectotympanic_ring" title="Ectotympanic ring">ectotympanic ring</a>, which supports the <a href="/wiki/Eardrum" title="Eardrum">eardrum</a>, free within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auditory_bulla" title="Auditory bulla">auditory bulla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191-97">[94]</a></sup> This trait is also seen in adapiforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006180_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006180-91">[88]</a></sup> In lorisoids, however, the tympanic cavity is smaller and the ectotympanic ring becomes attached to the edge of the auditory bulla. The tympanic cavity in lorisoids also has two accessory air spaces, which are not present in lemurs. Both lorisoids and <a href="/wiki/Cheirogaleidae" title="Cheirogaleidae">cheirogaleid</a> lemurs have replaced the <a href="/wiki/Internal_carotid_artery" title="Internal carotid artery">internal carotid artery</a> with an enlarged <a href="/wiki/Ascending_pharyngeal_artery" title="Ascending pharyngeal artery">ascending pharyngeal artery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191-97">[94]</a></sup> Strepsirrhines also possess distinctive features in their <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> (ankle bones) that differentiate them from haplorhines, such as a sloping talo-fibular facet (the face where the <a href="/wiki/Talus_bone" title="Talus bone">talus bone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a> meet) and a difference in the location of the position of the <a href="/wiki/Flexor_hallucis_longus_muscle" title="Flexor hallucis longus muscle">flexor fibularis</a> tendon on the talus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDagosto198847.E2.80.9348_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDagosto198847.E2.80.9348-98">[95]</a></sup> These differences give strepsirrhines the ability to make more complex rotations of the ankle and indicate that their feet are habitually inverted, or turned inward, an adaptation for grasping vertical supports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDagosto198849_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDagosto198849-99">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sexual dichromatism (different coloration patterns between males and females) can be seen in most brown lemur species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200768_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200768-100">[97]</a></sup> but otherwise lemurs show very little if any difference in body size or weight between sexes. This lack of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> is not characteristic of all strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001323_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001323-101">[98]</a></sup> Some adapiforms were sexually dimorphic, with males bearing a larger <a href="/wiki/Sagittal_crest" title="Sagittal crest">sagittal crest</a> (a ridge of bone on the top of the skull to which jaw muscles attach) and canine teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006168_.26_180_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006168_.26_180-102">[99]</a></sup> Lorisoids exhibit some sexual dimorphism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001323_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001323-101">[98]</a></sup> but males are typically no more than 20 percent larger than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlavcan2004239_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlavcan2004239-103">[100]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhinarium_and_olfaction">Rhinarium and olfaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rhinarium and olfaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prosimian_noses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1283" data-file-width="1202" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/Prosimian_noses.jpg/220px-Prosimian_noses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/Prosimian_noses.jpg/330px-Prosimian_noses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/Prosimian_noses.jpg/440px-Prosimian_noses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prosimian_noses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The noses of five prosimian primates: A) <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_lemur" title="Dwarf lemur">dwarf lemur</a>, B) <a href="/wiki/Greater_galago" title="Greater galago">greater galago</a>, C) <a href="/wiki/Lesser_bushbaby" title="Lesser bushbaby">lesser galago</a>, D) <a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">aye-aye</a>, and E) a <a href="/wiki/Tarsier" title="Tarsier">tarsier</a><br/>
A through D possess a rhinarium and are strepsirrhines, whereas E does not and is a haplorhine.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Strepsirrhines have a long snout that ends in a moist and touch-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">rhinarium</a>, similar to that of dogs and many other mammals. The rhinarium is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">vibrissae</a> that are also sensitive to touch. Convoluted maxilloturbinals on the inside of their nose filter, warm, and moisten the incoming air, while <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_receptor" title="Olfactory receptor">olfactory receptors</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_system" title="Olfactory system">main olfactory system</a> lining the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_concha" title="Nasal concha">ethmoturbinals</a> detect airborne smells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007400_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007400-104">[101]</a></sup> The olfactory bulbs of lemurs are comparable in size to those of other arboreal mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The surface of the rhinarium does not have any olfactory receptors, so it is not used for smell in terms of detecting <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> substances. Instead, it has sensitive touch receptors (<a href="/wiki/Merkel_cell" title="Merkel cell">Merkel cells</a>). The rhinarium, upper lip, and gums are tightly connected by a fold of <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membrane</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Philtrum" title="Philtrum">philtrum</a>, which runs from the tip of the nose to the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93394_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93394-105">[102]</a></sup> The upper lip is constrained by this connection and has fewer nerves to control movement, which leaves it less mobile than the upper lips of simians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007396_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007396-107">[104]</a></sup> The philtrum creates a gap (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diastema_(dentistry)" title="Diastema (dentistry)">diastema</a>) between the roots of the first two upper incisors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93394_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93394-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeard198885_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeard198885-108">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strepsirrhine rhinarium can collect relatively non-volatile, fluid-based chemicals (traditionally categorized as <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a>) and transmit them to the <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a> (VNO),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393_.26_400.E2.80.93401_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393_.26_400.E2.80.93401-109">[106]</a></sup> which is located below and in front of the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_cavity" title="Nasal cavity">nasal cavity</a>, above the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393-110">[107]</a></sup> The VNO is an encased <a href="/wiki/Duct_(anatomy)" title="Duct (anatomy)">duct</a>-like structure made of <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007402_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007402-111">[108]</a></sup> and is isolated from the air passing through the nasal cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007410_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007410-112">[109]</a></sup> The VNO is connected to the mouth through nasopalatine ducts (which communicate via the <a href="/wiki/Incisive_foramen" title="Incisive foramen">incisive foramen</a>), which pass through the <a href="/wiki/Hard_palate" title="Hard palate">hard palate</a> at the top, front of the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007392.E2.80.93393-110">[107]</a></sup> Fluids traveling from the rhinarium to the mouth and then up the nasopalatine ducts to the VNO are detected, and information is relayed to the <a href="/wiki/Olfaction#Accessory_olfactory_system" title="Olfaction">accessory olfactory bulb</a>, which is relatively large in strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007401_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007401-113">[110]</a></sup> From the accessory olfactory bulb, information is sent to the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, which handles emotions, and then to the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>, which handles basic body functions and metabolic processes. This neural pathway differs from that used by the main olfactory system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007410.E2.80.93411_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007410.E2.80.93411-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>All lemuriforms have a VNO, as do tarsiers and some New World monkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926-115">[112]</a></sup> Adapiforms exhibit the gap between the upper incisors, which indicates the presence of a VNO, but there is some disagreement over whether or not they possessed a rhinarium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006181.E2.80.93182_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006181.E2.80.93182-116">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_physiology">Reproductive physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reproductive physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Extant strepsirrhines have an epitheliochorial placenta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> where the maternal blood does not come in direct contact with the fetal <a href="/wiki/Chorion" title="Chorion">chorion</a> like it does in the <a href="/wiki/Placentation#Placentation_in_mammals" title="Placentation">hemochorial placenta</a> of haplorhines. The strepsirrhine <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a> has two distinct chambers (bicornuate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup> Despite having similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestation_period" title="Gestation period">gestation periods</a> to comparably sized haplorhines, fetal growth rates are generally slower in strepsirrhines, which results in newborn offspring that are as little as one-third the size of haplorhine newborns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325.E2.80.93326_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325.E2.80.93326-117">[114]</a></sup> Extant strepsirrhines also have a lower <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">basal metabolic rate</a>, which elevates in females during gestation, putting greater demands on the mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001330.E2.80.93331_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001330.E2.80.93331-118">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most primates have two <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201188_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201188-119">[116]</a></sup> but the number and positions vary between species within strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195381_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195381-120">[117]</a></sup> Lorises have two pairs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195393_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195393-121">[118]</a></sup> while others, like the ring-tailed lemur, have one pair on the chest (pectoral).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953391_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953391-122">[119]</a></sup> The aye-aye also has two mammary glands, but they are located near the groin (inguinal).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953672_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953672-123">[120]</a></sup> In females, the <a href="/wiki/Clitoris" title="Clitoris">clitoris</a> is sometimes enlarged and pendulous, <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-penis" title="Pseudo-penis">resembling the male penis</a>, which can make gender identification difficult for human observers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007523_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007523-124">[121]</a></sup> The clitoris may also have a bony structure in it, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Baculum" title="Baculum">baculum</a> (penis bone) in males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007522_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007522-125">[122]</a></sup> Most male primates have a baculum, but it is typically larger in strepsirrhines and usually forked at the tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007521_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007521-126">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="997" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/170px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/255px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/340px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Like other primates, infants often cling to their mother's fur.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Approximately three-quarters of all extant strepsirrhine species are nocturnal, sleeping in nests made from dead leaves or tree hollows during the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936-127">[124]</a></sup> All of the lorisoids from continental Africa and Asia are nocturnal, a circumstance that minimizes their competition with the simian primates of the region, which are diurnal. The lemurs of Madagascar, living in the absence of simians, are more variable in their activity cycles. The aye-aye, <a href="/wiki/Mouse_lemur" title="Mouse lemur">mouse lemurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woolly_lemur" title="Woolly lemur">woolly lemurs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sportive_lemur" title="Sportive lemur">sportive lemurs</a> are nocturnal, while <a href="/wiki/Ring-tailed_lemur" title="Ring-tailed lemur">ring-tailed lemurs</a> and most of their kin, <a href="/wiki/Sifaka" title="Sifaka">sifakas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indri" title="Indri">indri</a> are diurnal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200218_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200218-80">[77]</a></sup> Yet some or all of the <a href="/wiki/True_lemur" title="True lemur">brown lemurs</a> (<i>Eulemur</i>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathemeral" title="Cathemeral">cathemeral</a>, which means that they may be active during the day or night, depending on factors such as temperature and <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19995_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19995-128">[125]</a></sup> Many extant strepsirrhines are well adapted for nocturnal activity due to their relatively large eyes; large, movable ears; sensitive tactile hairs; strong sense of smell; and the tapetum lucidum behind the retina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.935_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.935-95">[92]</a></sup> Among the adapiforms, most are considered diurnal, with the exception of <i><a href="/wiki/Pronycticebus" title="Pronycticebus">Pronycticebus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Godinotia" title="Godinotia">Godinotia</a></i> from Middle Eocene Europe, both of which had large orbits that suggest nocturnality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200218_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200218-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006180_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006180-91">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reproduction in most strepsirrhine species tends to be seasonal, particularly in lemurs. Key factors that affect seasonal reproduction include the length of the wet season, subsequent food availability, and the maturation time of the species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325_.26_335_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325_.26_335-129">[126]</a></sup> Like other primates, strepsirrhines are relatively slow breeders compared to other mammals. Their gestation period and interbirth intervals are usually long, and the young develop slowly, just like in haplorhine primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325.E2.80.93326_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001325.E2.80.93326-117">[114]</a></sup> Unlike simians, some strepsirrhines produce two or three offspring, although some produce only a single offspring. Those that produce multiple offspring tend to build nests for their young. These two traits are thought to be <a href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">plesiomorphic</a> (ancestral) for primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup> The young are <a href="/wiki/Precocial" title="Precocial">precocial</a> (relatively mature and mobile) at birth, but not as coordinated as <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> (hoofed mammals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> Infant care by the mother is relatively prolonged compared to many other mammals, and in some cases, the infants cling to the mother's fur with their hands and feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325_106-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199924.E2.80.9325-106">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite their relatively smaller brains compared to other primates, lemurs have demonstrated levels of technical intelligence in problem solving that are comparable to those seen in simians. However, their social intelligence differs, often emphasizing within-group competition over cooperation, which may be due to adaptations for their unpredictable environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009395.E2.80.93396_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009395.E2.80.93396-130">[127]</a></sup> Although lemurs have not been observed using objects as <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">tools in the wild</a>, they can be trained to use objects as tools in captivity and demonstrate a basic understanding about the functional properties of the objects they are using.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009401.E2.80.93402_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009401.E2.80.93402-131">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_systems_and_communication">Social systems and communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Social systems and communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The nocturnal strepsirrhines have been traditionally described as "solitary", although this term is no longer favored by the researchers who study them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201151_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201151-132">[129]</a></sup> Many are considered "solitary foragers", but many exhibit complex and diverse social organization, often overlapping <a href="/wiki/Home_range" title="Home range">home ranges</a>, initiating social contact at night, and sharing sleeping sites during the day. Even the mating systems are variable, as seen in wooly lemurs, which live in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monogamous_pairing_in_animals" title="Monogamous pairing in animals">monogamous</a> <a href="/wiki/Breeding_pair" title="Breeding pair">breeding pairs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926.E2.80.9327_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926.E2.80.9327-133">[130]</a></sup> Because of this social diversity among these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solitary_but_social" title="Solitary but social">solitary but social</a> primates, whose level of social interaction is comparable to that of diurnal simians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201151_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201151-132">[129]</a></sup> alternative classifications have been proposed to emphasize their gregarious, dispersed, or solitary nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGouldSautherCameron201174_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGouldSautherCameron201174-134">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among extant strepsirrhines, only the diurnal and cathemeral lemurs have evolved to live in <a href="/wiki/Multi-male_group" title="Multi-male group">multi-male/multi-female groups</a>, comparable to most living simians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926.E2.80.9327_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199926.E2.80.9327-133">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009395_.26_397_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009395_.26_397-135">[132]</a></sup> This social trait, seen in two extant lemur families (<a href="/wiki/Indriidae" title="Indriidae">Indriidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lemuridae" title="Lemuridae">Lemuridae</a>), is thought to have evolved independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009398_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelKappeler2009398-136">[133]</a></sup> Group sizes are smaller in social lemurs than in simians, and despite the similarities, the community structures differ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199915.E2.80.9316_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199915.E2.80.9316-137">[134]</a></sup> Female dominance, which is rare in simians, is fairly common in lemurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOverdorffTecot2006247_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOverdorffTecot2006247-138">[135]</a></sup> Strepsirrhines spend a considerable amount of time grooming each other (<a href="/wiki/Social_grooming" title="Social grooming">allogrooming</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECloningerKedia201186_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECloningerKedia201186-139">[136]</a></sup> When lemuriform primates groom, they lick the fur and then comb it with their toothcomb. They also use their grooming claw to scratch places they cannot reach with their mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill195396-84">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like New World monkeys, strepsirrhines rely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scent_marking" title="Scent marking">scent marking</a> for much of their communication. This involves smearing secretions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)">epidermal scent glands</a> on tree branches, along with <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>. In some cases, strepsirrhines may anoint themselves with urine (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urine_washing" title="Urine washing">urine washing</a>). Body postures and gestures may be used, although the long snout, non-mobile lips, and reduced facial enervation restrict the use of facial expressions in strepsirrhines. Short-range calls, long-range calls, and <a href="/wiki/Alarm_signal" title="Alarm signal">alarm calls</a> are also used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199925.E2.80.9326_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199925.E2.80.9326-140">[137]</a></sup> Nocturnal species are more constrained by the lack of light, so their communication systems differ from those of diurnal species, often using long-range calls to claim their <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19995_.26_26_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19995_.26_26-141">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Locomotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:144px;max-width:144px">

</div>
</div>
<p>Living strepsirrhines are predominantly arboreal, with only the ring-tailed lemur spending considerable time on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup> Most species move around <a href="/wiki/Quadrupedalism" title="Quadrupedalism">quadrupedally</a> (on four legs) in the trees, including five genera of smaller, nocturnal lemurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936-127">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup> Galagos, indriids, sportive lemurs, and <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_lemur" title="Bamboo lemur">bamboo lemurs</a> leap from vertical surfaces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup> and the indriids are highly specialized for <a href="/wiki/Vertical_clinging_and_leaping" title="Vertical clinging and leaping">vertical clinging and leaping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant19994.E2.80.936-127">[124]</a></sup> Lorises are slow-moving, deliberate climbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analyses of extinct adapiforms postcranial skeletons suggest a variety of locomotor behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup> The European adapids <i>Adapis</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeolemur" title="Palaeolemur">Palaeolemur</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Leptadapis" title="Leptadapis">Leptadapis</a></i> shared adaptations for slow climbing like the lorises, although they may have been quadrupedal runners like small New World monkeys. Both <i><a href="/wiki/Notharctus" title="Notharctus">Notharctus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Smilodectes" title="Smilodectes">Smilodectes</a></i> from North America and <i><a href="/wiki/Europolemur" title="Europolemur">Europolemur</a></i> from Europe exhibit limb proportions and joint surfaces comparable to vertical clinging and leaping lemurs, but were not as specialized as indriids for vertical clinging, suggesting that they ran along branches and did not leap as much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006180_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006180-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup> Notharctids <i>Cantius</i> and <i>Pronycticebus</i> appear to have been agile arboreal quadrupeds, with adaptations comparable to the brown lemurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317_142-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216.E2.80.9317-142">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Primates primarily feed on fruits (including seeds), leaves (including flowers), and animal prey (<a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, small vertebrates, and eggs). Diets vary markedly between strepsirrhine species. Like other leaf-eating (<a href="/wiki/Folivore" title="Folivore">folivorous</a>) primates, some strepsirrhines can digest <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemicellulose" title="Hemicellulose">hemicellulose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199922.E2.80.9324_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199922.E2.80.9324-143">[140]</a></sup> Some strepsirrhines, such as the galagos, <a href="/wiki/Slender_loris" title="Slender loris">slender lorises</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angwantibo" title="Angwantibo">angwantibos</a>, are primarily <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivorous</a>. Other species, such as <a href="/wiki/Fork-marked_lemur" title="Fork-marked lemur">fork-marked lemurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Needle-clawed_bushbaby" title="Needle-clawed bushbaby">needle-clawed bushbabies</a>, specialize on tree gum, while indriids, sportive lemurs, and bamboo lemurs are folivores. Many strepsirrhines are <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivores</a> (fruit eaters), and others, like the ring-tailed lemur and mouse lemurs, are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, eating a mix of fruit, leaves, and animal matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216-144">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the adapiforms, frugivory seems to have been the most common diet, particularly for medium-sized to large species, such as <i>Cantius</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pelycodus" title="Pelycodus">Pelycodus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Cercamonius" title="Cercamonius">Cercamonius</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216-144">[141]</a></sup> Folivory was also common among the medium and large-sized adapiforms, including <i>Smilodectes</i>, <i>Notharctus</i>, <i>Adapis</i> and <i>Leptadapis</i>. Sharp <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cusp_(dentistry)" title="Cusp (dentistry)">cusps</a> on the teeth of some of the smaller adapiforms, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Anchomomys" title="Anchomomys">Anchomomys</a></i> and <i>Donrussellia</i>, indicate that they were either partly or primarily insectivorous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006180_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006180-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECovert200216_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECovert200216-144">[141]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before their extinction, adapiform primates were primarily found across North America, Asia, and Europe, with a few species in Africa. They flourished during the Eocene when those regions were more tropical in nature, and they disappeared when the climate became cooler and drier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGebo200235_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGebo200235-34">[33]</a></sup> Today, the lemuriforms are confined in the tropics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199914_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199914-145">[142]</a></sup> ranging between 28° S to 26° N latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhittenBrockman2001322-14">[13]</a></sup> Lorises are found both in <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Africa" title="Equatorial Africa">equatorial Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, while the galagos are limited to the forests and woodlands of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201191-97">[94]</a></sup> Lemurs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemic</a> to Madagascar, although much of their diversity and habitat has been lost due to recent human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201129-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>As with nearly all primates, strepsirrhines typically reside in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>. These habitats allow strepsirrhines and other primates to evolve diverse communities of <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatric species</a>. In the eastern rainforests of Madagascar, as many as 11 or 12 species share the same forests, and prior to human arrival, some forests had nearly double that diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199914_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199914-145">[142]</a></sup> Several species of lemur are found in drier, seasonal forests, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madagascar_spiny_thickets" title="Madagascar spiny thickets">spiny forest</a> on the southern tip of the island, although the lemur communities in these regions are not as rich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199919_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199919-146">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koh_Kong_logging.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Koh_Kong_logging.JPG/220px-Koh_Kong_logging.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Koh_Kong_logging.JPG/330px-Koh_Kong_logging.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Koh_Kong_logging.JPG/440px-Koh_Kong_logging.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koh_Kong_logging.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Strepsirrhines are threatened by <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in tropical regions.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_slow_lorises" title="Conservation of slow lorises">Conservation of slow lorises</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lemur#Conservation" title="Lemur">Lemur § Conservation</a></div>
<p>Like all other non-human primates, strepsirrhines face an elevated risk of extinction due to human activity, particularly <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in tropical regions. Much of their habitat has been converted for human use, such as agriculture and pasture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201184_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201184-20">[19]</a></sup> The threats facing strepsirrhine primates fall into three main categories: <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, hunting (for <a href="/wiki/Bushmeat" title="Bushmeat">bushmeat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a>), and live capture for export or local <a href="/wiki/Exotic_pet" title="Exotic pet">exotic pet</a> trade. Although hunting is often prohibited, the laws protecting them are rarely enforced. In Madagascar, local taboos known as <i><a href="/wiki/Fady_(taboo)" title="Fady (taboo)">fady</a></i> sometimes help protect lemur species, although some are still hunted for traditional medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199932.E2.80.9334_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMittermeierRylandsKonstant199932.E2.80.9334-147">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) announced that lemurs were the "most endangered mammals", due largely to elevated <a href="/wiki/Illegal_logging_in_Madagascar" title="Illegal logging in Madagascar">illegal logging</a> and hunting following a <a href="/wiki/2009_Malagasy_political_crisis" title="2009 Malagasy political crisis">political crisis in 2009</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_13Jul2012_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_13Jul2012-148">[145]</a></sup> In Southeast Asia, slow lorises are threatened by the exotic pet trade and traditional medicine, in addition to habitat destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008-149">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878-150">[147]</a></sup> Both lemurs and <a href="/wiki/Slow_loris" title="Slow loris">slow lorises</a> are protected from commercial international trade under <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES Appendix I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CITES_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-CITES-151">[148]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Strepsirrhini&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Lemuriformes-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lemuriformes_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lemuriformes_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic relationship</a> between lemurs and lorisoids is widely accepted, their <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> name is not. The term <i>lemuriform</i> is used here because it derives from one popular taxonomy that clumps the clade of toothcombed primates into one <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">infraorder</a> and the extinct, non-toothcombed <a href="/wiki/Adapiformes" title="Adapiformes">adapiforms</a> into another, both within the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">suborder</a> Strepsirrhini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESzalayDelson1980149_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESzalayDelson1980149-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmill201015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmill201015-2">[2]</a></sup> However, another popular alternative taxonomy places the <a href="/wiki/Lorisoidea" title="Lorisoidea">lorisoids</a> in their own infraorder, Lorisiformes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120.E2.80.9321_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartwig201120.E2.80.9321-3">[3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since they are thought to be close relatives of tarsiers, omomyiforms are classified as haplorhines. However, the spacing of the roots of their upper incisors suggests that they may have had a rhinarium, like the strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeard198892_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeard198892-57">[56]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Frequent mentions of a "strepsirrhine toothcomb" or references to Strepsirrhini as being "toothcombed primates" can be found in the literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaughanRyanCzaplewski2011169-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECartmillSmith201189-48">[47]</a></sup> However, one group of strepsirrhines lacks the toothcomb—the adapiforms. The toothcomb is therefore the primary hallmark of the lemuriforms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERose2006182_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006182-35">[34]</a></sup> although at least one family only retains it in modified form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007257_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007257-60">[58]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
